</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Close_relationships" title="Template:Close relationships"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Close_relationships" title="Template talk:Close relationships"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Close_relationships&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the context of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>, a <b>family</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">familia</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Social_group" title="Social group">group</a> of <a href="/wiki/People" title="People">people</a> related either by <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguinity</a> (by recognized birth), <a href="/wiki/Affinity_(law)" title="Affinity (law)">affinity</a> (by marriage or other relationship), or co-residence (as implied by the etymology of the English word "family")<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or some combination of these.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The purpose of families is to maintain the well-being of its members and of society. Ideally, families would offer predictability, structure, and safety as members mature and participate in the community.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In most societies, it is within families children acquire <a href="/wiki/Socialization" title="Socialization">socialization</a> for life outside the family. Additionally, as the basic unit for meeting the basic needs of its members, it provides a sense of boundaries for performing tasks in a heterosexual environment, ideally builds a person into a functional adult, transmits culture, and ensures continuity of humankind with precedents of knowledge.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anthropologist" title="Anthropologist">Anthropologists</a> generally classify most family organizations as <a href="/wiki/Matrifocal_family" title="Matrifocal family">matrifocal</a> (a mother and her children); conjugal (a wife, her husband, and children, also called the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a>); <a href="/wiki/Avuncular" class="mw-redirect" title="Avuncular">avuncular</a> (for example, a grandparent, a brother, his sister, and her children); or <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended</a> (parents and children co-reside with other members of one parent's family).
</p><p>Members of the <a href="/wiki/Immediate_family" title="Immediate family">immediate family</a> may include spouses, parents, grandparents, brothers, sisters, sons, and daughters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Members of the extended family may include aunts, uncles, cousins, nephews, nieces, and <a href="/wiki/Sibling-in-law" title="Sibling-in-law">siblings-in-law</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Sometimes these are also considered members of the immediate family, depending on an individual's specific relationship with them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Sexual relations among the members are regulated by rules concerning <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Incest_taboo" title="Incest taboo">incest taboo</a>.
</p><p>The word "families" can be used metaphorically to create more inclusive categories such as <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nationhood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_village_(term)" class="mw-redirect" title="Global village (term)">global village</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a>.
</p><p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogy</a> aims to trace family lineages through history.
</p><p>The family is also an important economic unit studied in <a href="/wiki/Family_economics" title="Family economics">family economics</a>.
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#The_concept_of_family_honor"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">The concept of family honor</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social">Social</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Social">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galla_Placidia_(rechts)_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg/220px-Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg/330px-Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg/440px-Galla_Placidia_%28rechts%29_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1407" data-file-height="1438" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galla_Placidia_(rechts)_und_ihre_Kinder.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a <a href="/wiki/Gold_glass" title="Gold glass">gold glass</a> medallion with a portrait of a family, from <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> (<a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>), 3rd–4th century (<a href="/wiki/Brescia" title="Brescia">Brescia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Museo_di_Santa_Giulia" class="mw-redirect" title="Museo di Santa Giulia">Museo di Santa Giulia</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg/220px-Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg/330px-Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg/440px-Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3631" data-file-height="4482" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sauk_Indian_family_by_Frank_Rinehart_1899.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sauk_people" title="Sauk people">Sauk</a> family of photographed by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Rinehart" title="Frank Rinehart">Frank Rinehart</a> in 1899</div></div></div>
<p>One of the primary functions of the family involves providing a framework for the production and reproduction of persons <a href="/wiki/Biologically" class="mw-redirect" title="Biologically">biologically</a> and socially. This can occur through the sharing of material substances (such as food); the giving and receiving of care and nurture (<a href="/wiki/Nurture_kinship" title="Nurture kinship">nurture kinship</a>); jural rights and obligations; and moral and sentimental ties.<sup id="cite_ref-Schneider_p._182_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider_p._182-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Thus, one's experience of one's family shifts over time. From the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Children" class="mw-redirect" title="Children">children</a>, the family is a "family of orientation": the family serves to locate children socially and plays a major role in their <a href="/wiki/Enculturation" title="Enculturation">enculturation</a> and socialization.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> From the point of view of the parent(s), the family is a "family of procreation", the goal of which is to produce, enculturate and socialize children.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> However, producing children is not the only function of the family; in societies with a sexual division of labor, <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a>, and the resulting relationship between two people, it is necessary for the formation of an economically productive <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/w/index.php?title=C._C._Harris_(writer)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="C. C. Harris (writer) (page does not exist)">Christopher Harris</a> notes that the western conception of family is ambiguous and confused with the <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a>, as revealed in the different contexts in which the word is used.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Harris" title="Olivia Harris">Olivia Harris</a> states this confusion is not accidental, but indicative of the familial ideology of <a href="/wiki/Capitalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitalist">capitalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">western</a> countries that pass social legislation that insists members of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> should live together, and that those not so related should not live together; despite the ideological and legal pressures, a large percentage of families do not conform to the ideal <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> type.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fertility_factor_(demography)" title="Fertility factor (demography)">Fertility factor (demography)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg/220px-Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg/330px-Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg/440px-Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg 2x" data-file-width="529" data-file-height="386" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mennonite_siblings_Montana_1937.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mennonite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mennonite">Mennonite</a> siblings, Montana, United States, 1937</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> of women varies from country to country, from a high of 6.76 children born/woman in <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> to a low of 0.81 in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> (as of 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Fertility is low in most <a href="/wiki/Eastern_European" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern European">Eastern European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_European" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern European">Southern European</a> countries; and high in most <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan African</a> countries.<sup id="cite_ref-cia.gov_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some cultures, the mother's preference of family size influences that of the children through early adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-AxinnClarkberg1994_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AxinnClarkberg1994-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> A parent's number of children strongly correlates with the number of children that their children will eventually have.<sup id="cite_ref-Murphy2013_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murphy2013-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_family"><span id="Family_types"></span>Types of family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types of family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001,_Berlin,_Passanten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001%2C_Berlin%2C_Passanten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="612" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-A0808-0008-001,_Berlin,_Passanten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mother with her children, Berlin, Germany, 1962</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wm._Riley_Blankinship,_miner,_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division,_Kopperston_Mine,_Kopperston,_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg/220px-Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg/330px-Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg/440px-Wm._Riley_Blankinship%2C_miner%2C_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division%2C_Kopperston_Mine%2C_Kopperston%2C_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2393" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wm._Riley_Blankinship,_miner,_with_his_children._Koppers_Coal_Division,_Kopperston_Mine,_Kopperston,_Wyoming_County..._-_NARA_-_540984.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A miner with his children</div></div></div>
<p>Although early western <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural anthropologist">cultural anthropologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sociologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Sociologist">sociologists</a> considered family and <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> to be universally associated with relations by "blood" (based on ideas common in their own cultures) later research<sup id="cite_ref-Schneider_p._182_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider_p._182-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> has shown that many societies instead understand family through ideas of living together, the sharing of food (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Milk_kinship" title="Milk kinship">milk kinship</a>) and sharing care and <a href="/wiki/Nurture_kinship" title="Nurture kinship">nurture</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sociologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Sociologist">Sociologists</a> have a special interest in the function and status of family forms in stratified (especially <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a>) societies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>According to the work of scholars <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Macfarlane" title="Alan Macfarlane">Alan Macfarlane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steven_Ozment" title="Steven Ozment">Steven Ozment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Goody" title="Jack Goody">Jack Goody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Laslett" title="Peter Laslett">Peter Laslett</a>, the huge transformation that led to modern marriage in Western democracies was "fueled by the religio-cultural value system provided by elements of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, early <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, Roman Catholic canon law and the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much sociological, <a href="/wiki/Historical" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical">historical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">anthropological</a> research dedicates itself to the understanding of this variation, and of changes in the family that form over time. Levitan claims:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"Times have changed; it is more acceptable and encouraged for mothers to work and fathers to spend more time at home with the children. The way roles are balanced between the parents will help children grow and learn valuable life lessons. There is [the] great importance of communication and equality in families, in order to avoid role strain."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span id="Conjugal_.28nuclear.29_family"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Conjugal_(nuclear)_family">Conjugal (nuclear) family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Conjugal (nuclear) family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a>" is commonly used, especially in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="United States of America">United States of America</a>, to refer to conjugal families. A "<a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">conjugal</a>" family includes only the spouses and unmarried children who are not of age.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2018)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Some sociologists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (June 2018)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> distinguish between conjugal families (relatively independent of the kindred of the parents and of other families in general) and nuclear families (which maintain relatively close ties with their kindred).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
Other family structures - with (for example)  <a href="/wiki/Blended_families" class="mw-redirect" title="Blended families">blended parents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Single_parent" title="Single parent">single parents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">domestic partnerships</a> – have begun to challenge the normality of the nuclear family.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Single-parent_family">Single-parent family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Single-parent family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <i><a href="/wiki/Single-parent_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Single-parent family">single-parent family</a></i> consists of one parent together with his or her children, where the parent is either widowed, divorced and not remarried, or never married. The parent may either have <a href="/wiki/Sole_custody" title="Sole custody">sole custody</a> of the children, or, the parents may have a <a href="/wiki/Shared_parenting" title="Shared parenting">shared parenting</a> arrangement, where the children divide their time equally between two different single-parent families or between one single-parent family and one <a href="/wiki/Blended_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Blended family">blended family</a>. Physical, mental and social well-being is lower for sole custody children, compared to children in nuclear families or shared parenting arrangements.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The number of single-parent families have been increasing, and about half of all children in the United States have lived in a single-parent family at some point before they reach the age of 18. Most single-parent families are headed by a mother, but the number of single-parent families headed by fathers is increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-Single_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Single-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrifocal_family">Matrifocal family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Matrifocal family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Matrifocal_family" title="Matrifocal family">Matrifocal family</a></div>
<p>A "matrifocal" family consists of a <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a> and her children. Generally, these children are her biological offspring, although adoption of children is a practice in nearly every society. This kind of family occurs commonly where women have the resources to rear their children by themselves, or where men are more mobile than women. As a definition, "a family or domestic group is matrifocal when it is centred on a woman and her children. In this case, the father(s) of these children are intermittently present in the life of the group and occupy a secondary place. The children's mother is not necessarily the wife of one of the children's fathers."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extended_family">Extended family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Extended family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg/220px-Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg/330px-Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg/440px-Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1747" data-file-height="1126" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Settled_sami_vasterbotten_sweden_publ_1926.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Settled Sami (Lapplander) family of farmers in <a href="/wiki/Stensele" title="Stensele">Stensele</a>, <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A4sterbotten" title="Västerbotten">Västerbotten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, early 20th century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg/220px-Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg/330px-Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg/440px-Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Basankusu_-_typical_fired_brick_house.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A family from Basankusu, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a>" is also common, especially in the United States. This term has two distinct meanings:
</p>
<ol><li>It serves as a synonym of "consanguinal family" (consanguine means "of the same blood").</li>
<li>In societies dominated by the conjugal family, it refers to "<a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kindred</a>" (an egocentric network of relatives that extends beyond the domestic group) who do not belong to the conjugal family.</li></ol>
<p>These types refer to ideal or normative structures found in particular societies. Any society will exhibit some variation in the actual composition and conception of families.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_of_choice">Family of choice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Family of choice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_family_of_choice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/A_family_of_choice.jpg/220px-A_family_of_choice.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/A_family_of_choice.jpg/330px-A_family_of_choice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/A_family_of_choice.jpg/440px-A_family_of_choice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="4032" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_family_of_choice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A family of choice in Brighton, England</div></div></div>
<p>The term "family of choice," also sometimes referred to as "chosen family," is common within the <a href="/wiki/LGBT_community" title="LGBT community">LGBT community</a>, both in academic literature and in colloquial vocabulary. It refers to the group of people in an individual's life that satisfies the typical role of family as a support system. The term differentiates between the "family of origin" (the biological family or that in which people are raised) and those that actively assume that ideal role.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
The family of choice may or may not include some or all of the members of the family of origin. This terminology stems from the fact that many <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> individuals, upon <a href="/wiki/Coming_out" title="Coming out">coming out</a>, face rejection or shame from the families they were raised in. The term family of choice is also used by individuals in the 12 step communities, who create close-knit "family" ties through the recovery process.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blended_family">Blended family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Blended family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <i>blended family</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Stepfamily" title="Stepfamily">stepfamily</a></i> describes families with mixed parents: one or both parents remarried, bringing children of the former family into the new family.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Also in sociology, particularly in the works of social psychologist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Lamb_(psychologist)" title="Michael Lamb (psychologist)">Michael Lamb</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <i>traditional family</i> refers to "a middle-class family with a bread-winning father and a stay-at-home mother, married to each other and raising their biological children," and <i>nontraditional</i> to exceptions from this rule. Most of the US households are now non-traditional under this definition.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Critics of the term "traditional family" point out that in most cultures and at most times, the <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a> model has been most common, not the nuclear family,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> though it has had a longer tradition in England<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> than in other parts of Europe and Asia which contributed large numbers of immigrants to the Americas.  The nuclear family became the most common form in the U.S. in the 1960s and 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of communication patterns in families, there are a certain set of beliefs within the family that reflect how its members should communicate and interact. These family communication patterns arise from two underlying sets of beliefs. One being conversation orientation (the degree to which the importance of communication is valued) and two, conformity orientation (the degree to which families should emphasize similarities or differences regarding attitudes, beliefs, and values).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monogamous_family">Monogamous family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Monogamous family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A monogamous family is based on a legal or social <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamy</a>. In this case, an individual has only one (official) partner during their lifetime or at any one time (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Monogamy#Serial_monogamy" title="Monogamy">serial monogamy</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-BRIT_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRIT-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> This means that a person may not have several different legal spouses at the same time, as this is usually prohibited by <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a> laws, in jurisdictions that require monogamous marriages.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polygamous_family">Polygamous family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Polygamous family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children,_Cairns,_1904_(9623512597).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg/220px-Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg/330px-Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg/440px-Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children%2C_Cairns%2C_1904_%289623512597%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="711" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kwong_Sue_Duk_with_his_three_wives_and_fourteen_children,_Cairns,_1904_(9623512597).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese immigrant with his three wives and fourteen children, <a href="/wiki/Cairns" title="Cairns">Cairns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, 1904</div></div></div> 
<p><a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">Polygamy</a> is a marriage that includes more than two partners.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zeitzen_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zeitzen-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> When a man is married to more than one wife at a time, the relationship is called <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a>; and when a woman is married to more than one husband at a time, it is called <a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">polyandry</a>. If a marriage includes multiple husbands and wives, it can be called <a href="/wiki/Polyamory" title="Polyamory">polyamory</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Group_marriage" title="Group marriage">group or conjoint marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zeitzen_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zeitzen-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">Polygyny</a> is a form of plural marriage, in which a man is allowed more than one wife .<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>  In modern countries that permit polygamy, polygyny is typically the only form permitted. Polygyny is practiced primarily (but not only) in parts of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>; and is often associated with <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, however, there are certain conditions in Islam that must be met to perform polygyny.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Polyandry" title="Polyandry">Polyandry</a> is a form of marriage whereby a woman takes two or more husbands at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-Starkweather/Hames_2012_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starkweather/Hames_2012-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Fraternal polyandry, where two or more brothers are married to the same wife, is a common form of polyandry. Polyandry was traditionally practiced in areas of the Himalayan mountains, among Tibetans in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, in parts of <a href="/wiki/Chin" title="Chin">China</a> and in parts of northern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. Polyandry is most common in societies marked by high male mortality or where males will often be apart from the rest of the family for a considerable period of time.<sup id="cite_ref-Starkweather/Hames_2012_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Starkweather/Hames_2012-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinship_terminology">Kinship terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Kinship terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Degrees_of_kinship">Degrees of kinship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Degrees of kinship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_relationship" title="Coefficient of relationship">Coefficient of relationship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg/200px-Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg/300px-Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg/400px-Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg 2x" data-file-width="421" data-file-height="570" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Albumen_photograph_of_an_Indian_family_with_children_in_the_1870s.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family in India, 1870s</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg/200px-Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg/300px-Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg/400px-Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1432" data-file-height="1164" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Rothstein_Family_in_a_wagon_Lee_County_August_1935.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family in a wagon, Lee County, Mississippi, United States, August 1935.</div></div></div>
<p>A first-degree relative is one who shares 50% of your DNA through direct inheritance, such as a full sibling, parent or progeny.
</p><p>There is another measure for the degree of relationship, which is determined by counting up generations to the first common ancestor and back down to the target individual, which is used for various genealogical and legal purposes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminologies">Terminologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Terminologies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

href="/wiki/File:Middagsm%C3%A5ltid_i_folkstugan._Oxie_h%C3%A4rad,_V%C3%A4stra_Klagstorps_socken,_Sk%C3%A5ne_-_Nordiska_Museet_-_NMA.0033405.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swedish family eating, 1902</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kinship_terminology" title="Kinship terminology">Kinship terminology</a></div>
<p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Systems_of_Consanguinity_and_Affinity_of_the_Human_Family" title="Systems of Consanguinity and Affinity of the Human Family">Systems of Consanguinity and Affinity of the Human Family</a></i>, anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Lewis_H._Morgan" title="Lewis H. Morgan">Lewis Henry Morgan</a> (1818–1881) performed the first survey of kinship terminologies in use around the world. Although much of his work is now considered dated, he argued that <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> terminologies reflect different sets of distinctions. For example, most kinship terminologies distinguish between sexes (the difference between a brother and a sister) and between generations (the difference between a child and a parent). Moreover, he argued, kinship terminologies distinguish between relatives by blood and <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> (although recently some anthropologists have argued that many societies define kinship in terms other than "blood").
</p><p>Morgan made a distinction between kinship systems that use <i>classificatory</i> terminology and those that use <i>descriptive</i> terminology. Classificatory systems are generally and erroneously understood to be those that "class together" with a single term relatives who actually do not have the same type of relationship to ego. (What defines "same type of relationship" under such definitions seems to be genealogical relationship. This is problematic given that any genealogical description, no matter how standardized, employs words originating in a folk understanding of kinship.) What Morgan's terminology actually differentiates are those (classificatory) kinship systems that do not distinguish lineal and collateral relationships and those (descriptive) kinship systems that do. Morgan, a lawyer, came to make this distinction in an effort to understand <a href="/wiki/Seneca_tribe" class="mw-redirect" title="Seneca tribe">Seneca</a> inheritance practices. A Seneca man's effects were inherited by his sisters' children rather than by his own children.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Morgan identified six basic patterns of kinship terminologies:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_kinship" title="Hawaiian kinship">Hawaiian</a>: only distinguishes relatives based upon sex and generation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_kinship" title="Sudanese kinship">Sudanese</a>: no two relatives share the same term.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eskimo_kinship" title="Eskimo kinship">Eskimo</a>: in addition to distinguishing relatives based upon sex and generation, also distinguishes between lineal relatives and collateral relatives.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iroquois_kinship" title="Iroquois kinship">Iroquois</a>: in addition to sex and generation, also distinguishes between siblings of opposite sexes in the parental generation.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crow_kinship" title="Crow kinship">Crow</a>: a matrilineal system with some features of an Iroquois system, but with a "skewing" feature in which generation is "frozen" for some relatives.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Omaha_kinship" title="Omaha kinship">Omaha</a>: like a Crow system but patrilineal.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Roles">Roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg/220px-Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg/330px-Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg/440px-Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2967" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Paus_family_portrait_NFB-18645.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group photograph of a Norwegian family by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Borgen" title="Gustav Borgen">Gustav Borgen</a> ca. 1900: Father, mother, three sons and two daughters.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloured-family.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coloured-family.jpg/220px-Coloured-family.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coloured-family.jpg/330px-Coloured-family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Coloured-family.jpg/440px-Coloured-family.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="630" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloured-family.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extended family with roots in Cape Town, Kimberley and Pretoria, South Africa</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen,_1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg/220px-Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg/330px-Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg/440px-Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen%2C_1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="677" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson_og_barnebarnet_Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Albert_Bj%C3%B8rnson-Langen,_1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rnstjerne_Bj%C3%B8rnson" title="Bjørnstjerne Bjørnson">Bjørnstjerne Bjørnson</a> with grandchild, 1900</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg/220px-Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg/330px-Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg/440px-Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3400" data-file-height="2350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baby_Mother_Grandmother_and_Great_Grandmother.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infant</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a>, his maternal <a href="/wiki/Grandmother" class="mw-redirect" title="Grandmother">grandmother</a>, and his <a href="/wiki/Great-grandmother" class="mw-redirect" title="Great-grandmother">great-grandmother</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/220px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/330px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png/440px-Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png 2x" data-file-width="1153" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Queen_Victoria_the_Princess_Royal_Victoria_c1844-5.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, with her eldest daughter</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Father_and_child,_Dhaka.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg/220px-Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg/330px-Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg/440px-Father_and_child%2C_Dhaka.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="1080" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Father_and_child,_Dhaka.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Father and child, Dhaka, Bangladesh</div></div></div>
<p>Most Western societies employ <a href="/wiki/Eskimo_kinship" title="Eskimo kinship">Eskimo kinship</a> terminology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This kinship terminology commonly occurs in societies based on <a href="/wiki/Conjugal_family" title="Conjugal family">conjugal</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear</a>) families, where nuclear families have a degree of relative mobility. Members of the nuclear use descriptive kinship terms:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Father" title="Father">Father</a></b>: a <a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">male</a> <a href="/wiki/Parent" title="Parent">parent</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">Mother</a></b>: a <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">female</a> parent.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Son" title="Son">Son</a></b>: a male <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">child</a> of the parent(s).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Daughter" title="Daughter">Daughter</a></b>: a female child of the parent(s).</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Brother" title="Brother">Brother</a></b>: a male <a href="/wiki/Sibling" title="Sibling">sibling</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Sister" title="Sister">Sister</a></b>: a female sibling.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Husband" title="Husband">Husband</a></b>: a male <a href="/wiki/Spouse" title="Spouse">spouse</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wife" title="Wife">Wife</a></b>: a female spouse.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Grandfather" class="mw-redirect" title="Grandfather">Grandfather</a></b>: the father of a parent.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Grandmother" class="mw-redirect" title="Grandmother">Grandmother</a></b>: the mother of a parent.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Cousin" title="Cousin">Cousins</a></b>: two people who share at least one <a href="/wiki/Grandparent" title="Grandparent">grandparent</a> in common, but none of the same parents.</li>
<li>(In some language there is a difference between a grandfather/grandmother from the father's side and one from the mother's side).</li></ul>
<p>Such systems generally assume that the mother's husband is also the biological father. In some families, a woman may have children with more than one man or a man may have children with more than one woman. The system refers to a child who shares only one parent with another child as a "half-brother" or "half-sister". For children who do not share biological or adoptive parents in common, English-speakers use the term "stepbrother" or "stepsister" to refer to their new relationship with each other when one of their biological parents marries one of the other child's biological parents. Any person (other than the biological parent of a child) who marries the parent of that child becomes the "stepparent" of the child, either the "stepmother" or "stepfather". The same terms generally apply to children adopted into a family as to children born into the family. In the United States, one in five mothers have children by different fathers; among mothers with two or more children the figure is higher, with 28% having children with at least two different men. Such families are more common among Blacks and Hispanics, and among the lower socioeconomic class.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Typically, societies with conjugal families also favor <a href="/wiki/Neolocal" class="mw-redirect" title="Neolocal">neolocal</a> residence; thus upon marriage, a person separates from the nuclear family of their childhood (family of orientation) and forms a new nuclear family (family of procreation). However, in western society, the single parent family has been growing more accepted and has begun to make an impact on culture. Single parent families are more commonly single mother families than single father. These families sometimes face difficult issues besides the fact that they have to rear their children on their own, for example, low income making it difficult to pay for rent, child care, and other necessities for a healthy and safe home. Members of the nuclear families of members of one's own (former) nuclear family may class as lineal or as collateral. Kin who regard them as lineal refer to them in terms that build on the terms used within the nuclear family:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Grandparent" title="Grandparent">Grandparent</a>
<ul><li><b>Grandfather</b>: a parent's father.</li>
<li><b>Grandmother</b>: a parent's mother.</li></ul></li>
<li>Grandchild
<ul><li><b>Grandson</b>: a child's son.</li>
<li><b>Granddaughter</b>: a child's daughter.</li></ul></li></ul>
<p>For collateral relatives, more classificatory terms come into play, terms that do not build on the terms used within the nuclear family:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Uncle" title="Uncle">Uncle</a></b>: parent's brother.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Aunt" title="Aunt">Aunt</a></b>: parent's sister.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Nephew" class="mw-redirect" title="Nephew">Nephew</a></b>: sibling's son.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Niece" class="mw-redirect" title="Niece">Niece</a></b>: sibling's daughter.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Nibling" class="mw-redirect" title="Nibling">Nibling</a></b>: sibling's child.</li></ul>
<p>When additional generations intervene (in other words, when one's collateral relatives belong to the same generation as one's grandparents or grandchildren), the prefixes "great-" or "grand-" modifies these terms. Also, as with grandparents and grandchildren, as more generations intervene the prefix becomes "great-grand-," adding another "great-" for each additional generation. Most collateral relatives have never had membership of the nuclear family of the members of one's own nuclear family.
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Cousin" title="Cousin">Cousin</a></b>: the most classificatory term; the children of uncles or aunts. One can further distinguish cousins by degrees of collaterality and by generation. Two persons of the same generation who share a grandparent count as "first cousins" (one degree of collaterality); if they share a great-grandparent they count as "second cousins" (two degrees of collaterality) and so on. If two persons share an ancestor, one as a grandchild and the other as a great-grandchild of that individual, then the two descendants class as "first cousins once removed" (removed by one generation); if they shared ancestor figures as the grandparent of one individual and the great-great-grandparent of the other, the individuals class as "first cousins twice removed" (removed by two generations), and so on. Similarly, if they shared ancestor figures as the great-grandparent of one person and the great-great-grandparent of the other, the individuals class as "second cousins once removed". Hence one can refer to a "third cousin once removed upwards."</li></ul>
<p>Cousins of an older generation (in other words, one's parents' first cousins), although technically first cousins once removed, are often classified with "aunts" and "uncles." English-speakers mark relationships by marriage (except for wife/husband) with the tag "-in-law." The mother and father of one's spouse become one's mother-in-law and father-in-law; the wife of one's son becomes one's daughter-in-law and the husband of one's daughter becomes one's son-in-law. The term "<a href="/wiki/Sister-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Sister-in-law">sister-in-law</a>" refers to three essentially different relationships, either the wife of one's brother, or the sister of one's spouse. "<a href="/wiki/Brother-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Brother-in-law">Brother-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's sister, or the brother of one's spouse. The terms "half-brother" and "half-sister" indicate siblings who share only one biological parent. The term "<a href="/wiki/Aunt-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Aunt-in-law">aunt-in-law</a>" is the wife of one's uncle, or the aunt of one's spouse. "<a href="/wiki/Uncle-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Uncle-in-law">Uncle-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's aunt, or the uncle of one's spouse. "<a href="/wiki/Cousin-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Cousin-in-law">Cousin-in-law</a>" is the spouse of one's cousin, or the cousin of one's spouse. The term "<a href="/wiki/Niece-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Niece-in-law">niece-in-law</a>" is the wife of one's nephew, or the niece of one's spouse. "<a href="/wiki/Nephew-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Nephew-in-law">Nephew-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's niece, or the nephew of one's spouse. The grandmother and grandfather of one's spouse become one's grandmother-in-law and grandfather-in-law; the wife of one's grandson becomes one's granddaughter-in-law and the husband of one's granddaughter becomes one's grandson-in-law. The term "co-sister-in-law" is the wife of one's brother of one's spouse. "<a href="/wiki/Co-brother-in-law" class="mw-redirect" title="Co-brother-in-law">Co-brother-in-law</a>" is the husband of one's sister of one's spouse. The term "co-aunt-in-law" is the wife of one's uncle of one's spouse. "Co-uncle-in-law" is the husband of one's aunt of one's spouse. "Co-cousin-in-law" is the spouse of one's cousin of one's spouse. The term "co-niece-in-law" is the wife of one's nephew of one's spouse. "Co-nephew-in-law" is the husband of one's niece of one's spouse. The mother and father of one's child-in-law become one's "co-mother-in-law" and "co-father-in-law".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_kinship">Types of kinship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Types of kinship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patrilineal">Patrilineal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Patrilineal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">Patrilineality</a>, also known as <i>the male line</i> or <i>agnatic kinship</i>, is a form of kinship system in which an individual's family membership derives from and is traced through his or her father's <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> It generally involves the <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> of property, rights, names, or titles by persons related through <a href="/wiki/Male" title="Male">male</a> kin.
</p><p>A patriline ("father line") is a person's father, and additional <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestors</a> that are traced only through males. One's patriline is thus a record of descent from a man in which the individuals in all intervening generations are male. In <a href="/wiki/Cultural_anthropology" title="Cultural anthropology">cultural anthropology</a>, a patrilineage is a <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguineal</a> male and female <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship group</a>, each of whose members is descended from the common ancestor through male forebears.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Matrilineal">Matrilineal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Matrilineal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Matrilineality" title="Matrilineality">Matrilineality</a> is a form of kinship system in which an individual's family membership derives from and is traced through his or her mother's <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>.
</p><p>It may also correlate with a <a href="/wiki/Societal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Societal system">societal system</a> in which each person is identified with their matriline—their mother's <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(anthropology)" title="Lineage (anthropology)">lineage</a>—and which can involve the <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> of property and titles. A matriline is a <a href="/wiki/Line_of_descent" class="mw-redirect" title="Line of descent">line of descent</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Female" title="Female">female</a> <a href="/wiki/Ancestor" title="Ancestor">ancestor</a> to a descendant in which the individuals in all intervening generations are mothers&#160;&#8211;&#32;in other words, a "mother line".
</p><p>In a matrilineal descent system, an individual is considered to belong to the same <a href="/wiki/Descent_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Descent group">descent group</a> as her or his mother. This matrilineal descent pattern is in contrasts to the more common pattern of patrilineal descent pattern.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bilateral_descent">Bilateral descent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bilateral descent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bilateral_descent" title="Bilateral descent">Bilateral descent</a> is a form of kinship system in which an individual's family membership derives from and is traced through both the paternal and maternal sides. The relatives on the mother's side and father's side are equally important for emotional ties or for transfer of property or wealth. It is a family arrangement where descent and inheritance are passed equally through both parents.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Families who use this system trace descent through both parents simultaneously and recognize multiple ancestors, but unlike with <a href="/wiki/Cognatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognatic">cognatic</a> descent it is not used to form descent groups.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, this is found among some groups in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a>. Anthropologists believe that a tribal structure based on bilateral descent helps members live in extreme environments because it allows individuals to rely on two sets of families dispersed over a wide area.<sup id="cite_ref-scientificamerican2001_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scientificamerican2001-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_theories">History of theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History of theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_family" title="History of the family">History of the family</a></div>
<p>Early scholars of family history applied <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwin</a>'s biological <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Theory of evolution">theory of evolution</a> in their theory of evolution of family systems.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> American anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Lewis_H._Morgan" title="Lewis H. Morgan">Lewis H. Morgan</a> published <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Society" title="Ancient Society">Ancient Society</a></i> in 1877 based on his theory of the three stages of human progress from <a href="/wiki/Primitive_culture" title="Primitive culture">Savagery</a> through <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">Barbarism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">Civilization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Morgan's book was the "inspiration for <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>' book" <i><a href="/wiki/The_Origin_of_the_Family,_Private_Property_and_the_State" title="The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State">The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State</a></i> published in 1884.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Engels expanded Morgan's hypothesis that economical factors caused the transformation of primitive community into a class-divided society.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Engels' theory of <a href="/wiki/Resource" title="Resource">resource</a> control, and later that of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>, was used to explain the cause and effect of change in family structure and function. The popularity of this theory was largely unmatched until the 1980s, when other sociological theories, most notably <a href="/wiki/Structural_functionalism" title="Structural functionalism">structural functionalism</a>, gained acceptance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_nuclear_family_in_industrial_society">The nuclear family in industrial society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The nuclear family in industrial society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Families_US.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Families_US.svg/300px-Families_US.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="252" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Families_US.svg/450px-Families_US.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Families_US.svg/600px-Families_US.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="530" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Families_US.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family arrangements in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> have become more diverse with no particular household arrangement representing half of the United States population.<sup id="cite_ref-Marriages,_Families_&amp;_Intimate_Relationships_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marriages,_Families_&amp;_Intimate_Relationships-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Contemporary society generally views the family as a haven from the world, supplying absolute fulfillment. Zinn and Eitzen discuss the image of the "family as  haven [...] a place of <a href="/wiki/Intimacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Intimacy">intimacy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Love" title="Love">love</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trust_(social_sciences)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust (social sciences)">trust</a> where individuals may <a href="/wiki/Escapism" title="Escapism">escape</a> the competition of dehumanizing forces in modern society".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
During <a href="/wiki/Industrialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a>, "[t]he family as a repository of warmth and tenderness (embodied by the mother) stands in opposition to the competitive and aggressive world of commerce (embodied by the father). The family's task was to protect against the outside world."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> However, Zinn and Eitzen note, "The protective image of the family has waned in recent years as the ideals of family fulfillment have taken shape. Today, the family is more compensatory than protective. It supplies what is vitally needed but missing in other social arrangements."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"The popular wisdom", according to Zinn and Eitzen, sees the family structures of the past as superior to those today, and families as more stable and happier at a time when they did not have to contend with problems such as illegitimate children and <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a>. They respond to this, saying, "there is no golden age of the family gleaming at us in the far back historical past."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> "Desertion by spouses, illegitimate children, and other conditions that are considered characteristics of modern times existed in the past as well."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_postmodern_family">The postmodern family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The postmodern family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Non_marital_by_countries.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Non_marital_by_countries.gif" decoding="async" width="440" height="262" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="262" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Non_marital_by_countries.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Percentage of births to unmarried women, selected countries, 1980 and 2007<sup id="cite_ref-non_mar1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-non_mar1-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Others argue that whether or not one views the family as "declining" depends on one's definition of "family". "Married couples have dropped below half of all American households. This drop is shocking from traditional forms of the family system. Only a fifth of households were following traditional ways of having married couples raising a family together."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In the Western World, marriages are no longer <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged</a> for economic, social or political gain, and children are no longer expected to contribute to family income. Instead, people choose mates based on <a href="/wiki/Love" title="Love">love</a>. This increased role of love indicates a societal shift toward favoring emotional fulfilment and relationships within a family, and this shift necessarily weakens the institution of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Margaret Mead considers the family as a main safeguard to continuing human progress. Observing, "Human beings have learned, laboriously, to be human", she adds: "we hold our present form of humanity on trust, [and] it is possible to lose it" ... "It is not without significance that the most successful large-scale abrogations of the family have occurred not among simple savages, living close to the subsistence edge, but among great nations and strong empires, the resources of which were ample, the populations huge, and the power almost unlimited"<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many countries (particularly Western) have, in recent years, changed their <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> in order to accommodate diverse family models. For instance, in the United Kingdom, in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the <i>Family Law (Scotland) Act 2006</i> provides cohabitants with some limited rights.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, Ireland enacted the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Partnership_and_Certain_Rights_and_Obligations_of_Cohabitants_Act_2010" title="Civil Partnership and Certain Rights and Obligations of Cohabitants Act 2010">Civil Partnership and Certain Rights and Obligations of Cohabitants Act 2010</a>.
