There have also been moves at an international level, most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>  <i>European Convention on the Legal Status of Children Born out of Wedlock</i><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> which came into force in 1978. Countries which ratify it must ensure that children born outside marriage are provided with legal rights as stipulated in the text of this Convention. The Convention was ratified by the UK in 1981 and by Ireland in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oedipal_family_model_and_fascism">Oedipal family model and fascism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Oedipal family model and fascism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Undue_weight plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-undue-weight" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/50px-Ambox_scales.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/75px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Ambox_scales.svg/100px-Ambox_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="620" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section may <b>lend <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Due_and_undue_weight" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">undue weight</a> to certain ideas, incidents, or controversies</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> by rewriting it in a <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Balance" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">balanced fashion</a></b> that contextualizes different points of view.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The model, common in the western societies, of the family triangle, husband-wife-children isolated from the outside, is also called the <i><a href="/wiki/Oedipal_model" class="mw-redirect" title="Oedipal model">oedipal model</a> of the family</i>, and it is a form of <a href="/wiki/Patriarchal" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchal">patriarchal</a> family. Many philosophers and psychiatrists have analyzed such a model.
In such a family, they argue, the young develop in a perverse relationship, wherein they learn to love the same person who beats and oppresses them. They believe that young children grow up and develop loving a person who is oppressing them physically or mentally, and that these children are not taught in a way that will raise affectionate children.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Such philosophers claim that the family therefore constitutes the first cell of the fascist society, as the children will carry this attitude of love for oppressive figures in their adult life.<sup id="cite_ref-FoucaultPreface_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoucaultPreface-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reich33GermCell_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reich33GermCell-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> They claim that fathers torment their sons.<sup id="cite_ref-Phillips_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reich36SexRev_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reich36SexRev-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Deleuze and Guattari, in their analysis of the dynamics at work within a family, "track down all varieties of fascism, from the enormous ones that surround and crush us to the petty ones that constitute the tyrannical bitterness of our everyday lives".<sup id="cite_ref-FoucaultPreface_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoucaultPreface-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As it has been explained by Deleuze, Guattari and Foucault, as well as other philosophers and psychiatrists such as <a href="/wiki/Ronald_David_Laing" class="mw-redirect" title="Ronald David Laing">Laing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Reich" title="Wilhelm Reich">Reich</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchal" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchal">patriarchal</a>-family conceived in the West tradition serves the purpose of perpetuating a <a href="/wiki/Propertarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Propertarian">propertarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Authoritarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> society.<sup id="cite_ref-Deleuze-Guattari72p129-31_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deleuze-Guattari72p129-31-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The child grows according to the oedipal model, which is typical of the structure of capitalist societies,<sup id="cite_ref-Lacan3801_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lacan3801-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FugierPascal226_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FugierPascal226-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and he becomes in turn owner of <a href="/wiki/Submissive" class="mw-redirect" title="Submissive">submissive</a> children and protector of the woman.<sup id="cite_ref-Reich36SexRev_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reich36SexRev-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-patriarchal_proprietary_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patriarchal_proprietary-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-luttazzi_patriarchal_family_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-luttazzi_patriarchal_family-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adorno_family_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adorno_family-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EJAnthony_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EJAnthony-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the young undergoes physical and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_repression" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychological repression">psychological repression</a> from someone for whom they develop love, they develop a loving attitude towards authority figures. They will bring such attitude in their adult life, when they will desire <a href="/wiki/Social_control" title="Social control">social repression</a> and will form docile subjects for society.<sup id="cite_ref-Deleuze-Guattari72p129-31_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deleuze-Guattari72p129-31-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a>, in his systematic study of <a href="/wiki/Sexology" title="Sexology">sexuality</a>, argued that rather than being merely repressed, the desires of the individual are efficiently mobilized and used,<sup id="cite_ref-FoucaultPreface_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoucaultPreface-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> to control the individual, alter <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationships" class="mw-redirect" title="Interpersonal relationships">interpersonal relationships</a> and control the masses. Foucault believed <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religion</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Moral" title="Moral">moral</a> <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">prohibitions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economic_power" title="Economic power">economic powers</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">advertising</a>, make use of unconscious sex drives. Dominating desire, they dominate individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> According to the analysis of <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Michel Foucault</a>, in the west:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the [conjugal] family organization, precisely to the extent that it was insular and heteromorphous with respect to the other <a href="/wiki/Power_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Power (sociology)">power</a> mechanisms, was used to support the great "maneuvers" employed for the <a href="/wiki/Malthusian" class="mw-redirect" title="Malthusian">Malthusian</a> control of the birthrate, for the populationist incitements, for the medicalization of sex and the psychiatrization of its nongenital forms.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>Michel Foucault, <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Sexuality" title="The History of Sexuality">The History of Sexuality</a></i> vol I, chap. IV, sect. <i>Method</i>, rule 3, p. 99</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_violence">Domestic violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Domestic violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">Domestic violence</a></div>
<p>Domestic violence (DV) is violence that happens within the family. The legal and social understanding of the concept of DV differs by culture. The definition of the term "domestic violence" varies, depending on the context in which it is used.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> It may be defined differently in medical, legal, political or social contexts. The definitions have varied over time, and vary in different parts of the world.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_preventing_and_combating_violence_against_women_and_domestic_violence" title="Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence">Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence</a> states that:<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>" “domestic violence” shall mean all acts of physical, sexual, psychological or economic violence that occur within the family or domestic unit or between former or current spouses or partners, whether or not the perpetrator shares or has shared the same residence with the victim".</dd></dl>
<p>In 1993, the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_Violence_against_Women" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration on the Elimination of Violence against Women">Declaration on the Elimination of Violence against Women</a> identified domestic violence as one of three contexts in which <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a> occurs, describing it as:<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"Physical, sexual and psychological violence occurring in the family, including battering, sexual abuse of female children in the household, <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>-related violence, <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> and other traditional practices harmful to women, non-spousal violence and violence related to exploitation".</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_violence">Family violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Family violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Family violence is a broader definition, often used to include <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">child abuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elder_abuse" title="Elder abuse">elder abuse</a>, and other violent acts between family members.<sup id="cite_ref-Wallace_p._2_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wallace_p._2-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Child abuse is defined by the WHO as:<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>"Child maltreatment, sometimes referred to as child abuse and neglect, includes all forms of physical and emotional ill-treatment, sexual abuse, neglect, and exploitation that results in actual or potential harm to the child’s health, development or dignity. Within this broad definition, five subtypes can be distinguished – physical abuse; sexual abuse; neglect and negligent treatment; emotional abuse; and exploitation." There exists legislation to prevent and punish the occurrence of these offences. There are laws regarding familial sexual activity, which states that it is a criminal offence to have any kind of sexual relationship between one's grandparent, parent, sibling, aunt or uncle.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Elder abuse is, according to the WHO: "a single, or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust which causes harm or distress to an older person".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Parental_abuse_of_children_.28child_abuse.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_abuse_of_children_(child_abuse)">Parental abuse of children (child abuse)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Parental abuse of children (child abuse)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parental_abuse_of_children" class="mw-redirect" title="Parental abuse of children">Parental abuse of children</a></div>
<p>Child abuse is the physical, sexual or emotional maltreatment or neglect of a child or children.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) and the Department for Children and Families (DCF) define child maltreatment as any act or series of acts of commission or omission by a parent or other caregiver that results in harm, potential for harm, or threat of harm to a child.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDC-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Child abuse can occur in a child's home, or in the organizations, schools or communities the child interacts with. There are four major categories of child abuse: <a href="/wiki/Child_neglect" title="Child neglect">neglect</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_abuse" title="Physical abuse">physical abuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychological_abuse" title="Psychological abuse">psychological or emotional abuse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Parental_abuse_by_children">Parental abuse by children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Parental abuse by children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parental_abuse_by_children" title="Parental abuse by children">Parental abuse by children</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abuse" title="Abuse">Abuse</a> of parents by their children is a common but under reported and under researched subject. Parents are quite often subject to levels of <a href="/wiki/Childhood_aggression" class="mw-redirect" title="Childhood aggression">childhood aggression</a> in excess of normal childhood aggressive outbursts, typically in the form of <a href="/wiki/Verbal_abuse" title="Verbal abuse">verbal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Physical_abuse" title="Physical abuse">physical</a> abuse. Parents feel a sense of <a href="/wiki/Shame" title="Shame">shame</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humiliation" title="Humiliation">humiliation</a> to have that problem, so they rarely seek help and there is usually little or no help available anyway.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Elder_abuse">Elder abuse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Elder abuse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elder_abuse" title="Elder abuse">Elder abuse</a></div>
<p>Elder abuse is "a single, or repeated act, or lack of appropriate action, occurring within any relationship where there is an expectation of trust, which causes harm or distress to an older person."<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This definition has been adopted by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> from a definition put forward by Action on Elder Abuse in the UK. Laws protecting the elderly from abuse are similar to, and related to, laws protecting <a href="/wiki/Dependent_adult" title="Dependent adult">dependent adults</a> from abuse.
</p><p>The core element to the harm of elder abuse is the "expectation of trust" of the older person toward their abuser. Thus, it includes harms by people the older person knows or with whom they have a relationship, such as a spouse, partner or family member, a friend or neighbor, or people that the older person relies on for services. Many forms of elder abuse are recognized as types of domestic violence or family violence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_and_child_marriage">Forced and child marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Forced and child marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">Forced marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">Child marriage</a></div>
<p>Forced and child marriages are practiced in certain regions of the world, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and these types of marriages are associated with a high rate of domestic violence.<sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a> is a marriage where one or both participants are married without their freely given consent.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The line between forced marriage and consensual marriage may become blurred, because the social norms of many cultures dictate that one should never oppose the desire of one's parents/relatives in regard to the choice of a spouse; in such cultures it is not necessary for violence, threats, intimidation etc. to occur, the person simply "consents" to the marriage even if he/she doesn't want it, out of the implied social pressure and duty. The customs of <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, that exist in parts of the world, can lead to buying and selling people into marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A child marriage is a <a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">marriage</a> where one or both spouses are under 18.<sup id="cite_ref-unicef.org_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unicef.org-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hrw.org_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hrw.org-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Child marriage was common throughout history but is today condemned by international human rights organizations.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-who.int_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Child marriages are often arranged between the families of the future bride and groom, sometimes as soon as the girl is born.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Child marriages can also occur in the context of <a href="/wiki/Marriage_by_abduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage by abduction">marriage by abduction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_concept_of_family_honor">The concept of family honor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: The concept of family honor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_honor" title="Family honor">Family honor</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_honor" class="mw-redirect" title="Culture of honor">Culture of honor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Honor_killing" title="Honor killing">Honor killing</a></div>
<p>Family honor is an abstract concept involving the perceived quality of worthiness and respectability that affects the social standing and the self-evaluation of a group of related people, both corporately and individually.<sup id="cite_ref-Malina2001_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malina2001-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-spain_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spain-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> The family is viewed as the main source of <a href="/wiki/Honor" class="mw-redirect" title="Honor">honor</a> and the community highly values the relationship between honor and the family.<sup id="cite_ref-Family_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Family-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The conduct of family members reflects upon family honor and the way the family perceives itself, and is perceived by others.<sup id="cite_ref-spain_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spain-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_honor" class="mw-redirect" title="Culture of honor">cultures of honor</a> maintaining the family honor is often perceived as more important than either <a href="/wiki/Individual_freedom" class="mw-redirect" title="Individual freedom">individual freedom</a>, or individual achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-McGoldrickGiordano2005_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGoldrickGiordano2005-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In extreme cases, engaging in acts that are deemed to tarnish the honor of the family results in <a href="/wiki/Honor_killing" title="Honor killing">honor killings</a>. An honor killing is the <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a> of a member of a family or social group by other members, due to the perpetrators' belief that the victim has brought shame or dishonor upon the family or community, usually for reasons such as refusing to enter an <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged marriage</a>, being in a relationship that is disapproved by their relatives, having <a href="/wiki/Sex_outside_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Sex outside marriage">sex outside marriage</a>, becoming the victim of <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, dressing in ways which are deemed inappropriate, or engaging in <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexual</a> relations.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_issues">Economic issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Economic issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A family is often part of a <a href="/wiki/Sharing_economy" title="Sharing economy">sharing economy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Common_ownership" title="Common ownership">common ownership</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Dowry.2C_bride_price_and_dower"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dowry,_bride_price_and_dower">Dowry, bride price and dower</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Dowry, bride price and dower">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">Dowry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">Bride price</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dower" title="Dower">Dower</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg/220px-Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg/330px-Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg/440px-Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4368" data-file-height="2912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thai_Bride_Price_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional, formal presentation of the bride price at a Thai engagement ceremony.</div></div></div>
<p>Dowry is property (money, goods, or estate) that a wife or wife's family gives to her husband when the wife and husband marry.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Offering dowry was common in many cultures historically (including in Europe and North America), but this practice today is mostly restricted to some areas primarily in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.
</p><p>Bride price, (also bridewealth or bride token), is property paid by the groom or his family to the parents of a woman upon the marriage of their daughter to the groom. It is practiced mostly in Sub-Saharan Africa, parts of South-East Asia (<a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>), and parts of Central Asia.
</p><p>Dower is property given to the bride herself by the groom at the time of marriage, and which remains under her ownership and control.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Property_regimes_and_taxation">Property regimes and taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Property regimes and taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some countries married couples benefit from various taxation advantages not available to a single person or to unmarried couples. For example, spouses may be allowed to average their combined incomes. Some jurisdictions recognize <a href="/wiki/Common_law_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Common law marriage">common law marriage</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> relations for this purposes. In some jurisdictions there is also an option of <a href="/wiki/Civil_partnership" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil partnership">civil partnership</a> or <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">domestic partnership</a>.
</p><p>Different property regimes exist for spouses. In many countries, each marriage partner has the choice of keeping their property <a href="/wiki/Community_property" title="Community property">separate</a> or combining properties. In the latter case, called <a href="/wiki/Community_property" title="Community property">community property</a>, when the marriage ends by divorce each owns half. In lieu of a <a href="/wiki/Will_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Will (law)">will</a> or <a href="/wiki/Trust_law" title="Trust law">trust</a>, property owned by the deceased generally is inherited by the surviving spouse.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rights_and_laws">Rights and laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Rights and laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_rights">Reproductive rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Reproductive rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive rights</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">Forced sterilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_pregnancy" title="Forced pregnancy">Forced pregnancy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">Forced abortion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/250px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/375px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/500px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="435" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries and territories by <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">fertility rate</a> as of 2018</div></div></div>
<p>Reproductive rights are legal rights and freedoms relating to reproduction and <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a>. These include the right to decide on issues regarding the number of children born, family planning, contraception, and private life, free from <a href="/wiki/Coercion" title="Coercion">coercion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">discrimination</a>; as well as the right to access health services and adequate information.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>  According to <a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a>, reproductive rights "include the right to decide the number, timing and spacing of children, the right to voluntarily marry and establish a family, and the right to the highest attainable standard of health, among others".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a> refers to the factors that may be considered by  individuals and couples in order for them to control their fertility, anticipate and attain the desired number of children and the spacing and timing of their births.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The state and church have been, and still are in some countries, involved in controlling the size of families, often using coercive methods, such as bans on contraception or abortion (where the policy is a <a href="/wiki/Natalist" class="mw-redirect" title="Natalist">natalist</a> one—for example through <a href="/wiki/Tax_on_childlessness" title="Tax on childlessness">tax on childlessness</a>) or conversely, discriminatory policies against large families or even <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">forced abortions</a> (e.g., China's <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a> in place from 1978 to 2015). <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">Forced sterilization</a> has often targeted ethnic minority groups, such as Roma women in Eastern Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> or indigenous women in Peru (during the 1990s).<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Parents.27_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parents'_rights">Parents' rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Parents&#039; rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Parents%27_rights_movement" title="Parents&#39; rights movement">parents' rights</a> movement is a movement whose members are primarily interested in issues affecting parents and children related to family law, specifically parental rights and obligations. <a href="/wiki/Mothers%27_rights" title="Mothers&#39; rights">Mothers' rights</a> movements focus on  <a href="/wiki/Maternal_health" title="Maternal health">maternal health</a>, workplace issues such as <a href="/wiki/Labor_rights" title="Labor rights">labor rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a>, and rights in <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Fathers%27_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Fathers&#39; rights">fathers' rights</a> movement is a movement whose members are primarily interested in issues related to <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Child_custody" title="Child custody">child custody</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_support" title="Child support">child support</a>, that affect fathers and their <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">children</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Collier2006_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collier2006-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Children.27s_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children's_rights">Children's rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Children&#039;s rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_rights" title="Children&#39;s rights">Children's rights</a></div>
<p>Children's rights are the human rights of children, with particular attention to the rights of special protection and care afforded to minors, including their right to association with both parents, their right to human identity, their right to be provided in regard to their other basic needs, and their right to be free from violence and abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_rights">Marriage rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Marriage rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage laws">Marriage laws</a></div>
<p>Each jurisdiction has its own <a href="/wiki/Marriage_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage laws">marriage laws</a>. These laws differ significantly from country to country; and these laws are often controversial. Areas of controversy include <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">women's rights</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_reforms">Legal reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Legal reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Legal reforms to <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> have taken place in many countries during the past few decades. These dealt primarily with <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> within marriage and with <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Divorce law">divorce laws</a>. Women have been given equal rights in marriage in many countries, reversing older family laws based on the dominant legal role of the husband. <a href="/wiki/Coverture" title="Coverture">Coverture</a>, which was enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> of England and the US for several centuries and throughout most of the 19th century, was abolished. In some European countries the changes that lead to gender equality were slower. The period of 1975–1979 saw a major overhaul of <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a> in countries such as Italy,<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Spain,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Austria,<sup id="cite_ref-Contemporary_Western_European_Feminism_pp._133_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contemporary_Western_European_Feminism_pp._133-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Contemporary_Western_European_Feminism_pp._133_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Contemporary_Western_European_Feminism_pp._133-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> West Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> and Portugal.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In 1978, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> passed the <i>Resolution (78) 37 on equality of spouses in civil law</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Among the last European countries to establish full <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> in marriage were Switzerland. In 1985, a referendum guaranteed women legal equality with men within marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> The new reforms came into force in January 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> In Greece, in 1983, legislation was passed guaranteeing equality between spouses, abolishing <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, and ending legal discrimination against illegitimate children.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> In 1981, Spain abolished the requirement that married women must have their husbands’ permission to initiate judicial proceedings<sup id="cite_ref-wbl.worldbank.org_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wbl.worldbank.org-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> the Netherlands,<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> and France <sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> in the 1980s. In recent decades, the <a href="/wiki/Marital_power" title="Marital power">marital power</a> has also been abolished in African countries that had this doctrine, but many African countries that were former <a href="/wiki/French_colonies" class="mw-redirect" title="French colonies">French colonies</a> still have discriminatory laws in their marriages regulations, such regulations originating in the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a> that has inspired these laws.<sup id="cite_ref-wbl.worldbank.org_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wbl.worldbank.org-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In some countries (predominantly Roman Catholic) divorce was legalized only recently (e.g.  Italy (1970), Portugal (1975), Brazil (1977), Spain (1981), Argentina (1987), Ireland (1996), Chile (2004) and Malta (2011)) although <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legal_separation" title="Legal separation">legal separation</a> were options. Philippines still does not allow divorce. (see <a href="/wiki/Divorce_law_by_country" title="Divorce law by country">Divorce law by country</a>). The laws pertaining to the situation of children born outside marriage have also been revised in many countries (see <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(family_law)" title="Legitimacy (family law)">Legitimacy (family law)</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/300px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/450px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/600px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global maternal mortality rate per 100 000 live births, (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_medicine">Family medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Family medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Family_medicine" title="Family medicine">Family medicine</a></div>
<p>Family medicine is a medical specialty devoted to comprehensive health care for people of all ages; it is based on knowledge of the patient in the context of the family and the community, emphasizing disease prevention and health promotion.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The importance of family medicine is being increasingly recognized.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births,_under-5,_world_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/300px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/450px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/600px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births,_under-5,_world_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World infant mortality rates in 2012<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal_mortality">Maternal mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Maternal mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternal mortality">Maternal mortality</a></div>
<p>Maternal mortality or maternal death is defined by WHO as "the death of a woman while pregnant or within 42 days of termination of pregnancy, irrespective of the duration and site of the pregnancy, from any cause related to or aggravated by the pregnancy or its management but not from accidental or incidental causes."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Historically, maternal mortality was a major cause of women's death. In recent decades, advances in healthcare have resulted in rates of maternal mortality having dropped dramatically, especially in Western countries. Maternal mortality however remains a serious problem in many African and Asian counties.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infant_and_child_mortality">Infant and child mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Infant and child mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">Child mortality</a></div>
<p>Infant mortality is the death of a child less than one year of age. Child mortality is the death of a child before the child's fifth birthday. Like maternal mortality, infant and child mortality were common throughout history, but have decreased significantly in modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG/250px-Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG/375px-Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG/500px-Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parents_with_child_Statue_Hrobakova_street_Bratislava.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parents with child statue, Hrobákova street, Petržalka, <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas,_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas%2C_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg/250px-The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas%2C_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas%2C_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="375" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Family_sculpture_by_Robert_Thomas,_Cardiff_Queen_Street_-_geograph.org.uk_-_624471.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Family</i>, a sculpture by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Thomas_(sculptor)" title="Robert Thomas (sculptor)">Robert Thomas</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Cardiff,_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiff, Wales">Cardiff, Wales</a></div></div></div>
<p>Family policies differ significantly between countries. Depending on jurisdiction, family policy may have a multiplicity of functions: horizontal redistribution, the enhancement of individual choices, increasing fertility rates, supporting economic growth and productivity, as well as reducing gender inequalities (Ferragina and Seeleib-Kaiser 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-sp.oxfordjournals.org_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sp.oxfordjournals.org-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> From a societal perspective, family policies can contribute to "horizontal redistribution" between generations, as well as between households with and without children; to favour individual choices by supporting the reconciliation between care and paid work; and to reduce the costs of having children and child poverty. From an economic perspective, employment-oriented family policy is part of an overall redesign of <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare states</a> geared to foster "active citizenship", also among mothers who were formerly not employed, through the development of an "enabling state". More generous family policies are said to lead to higher employment rates for women, mitigate the risk of unemployment for mothers after a substantial period of leave, support a social investment strategy, and offset some of the costs of raising children. From many feminist perspectives, family policies should aim at equalising opportunities between men and women through de-familialising care, encouraging men's involvement in care work, and facilitating employment opportunities for women. Profound social, economic, and cultural changes have led in many societies to the decline of the "male breadwinner model" and the move towards a variety of "adult worker models" (Daly 2011). Nevertheless, family policy expansion has not always fundamentally challenged gender inequalities: overall men have not increased their contribution to care work sufficiently to "compensate" for women's increased labour force participation and slightly reduced participation in care.
</p><p>In an era of perceived permanent austerity and overall welfare state retrenchment, rich OECD countries have not been prevented from expanding family policies (Ferragina and Seeleib-Kaiser 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-sp.oxfordjournals.org_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sp.oxfordjournals.org-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In fact, in many of these countries there has been an expansion of family policies, leading to a socialisation of family care responsibilities, traditionally disproportionately performed by women (Daly and Lewis 2000). Although at the institutional policy level, the expansion of family policy might be characterised as a "silent revolution", relevant for gender equality, a cautious interpretation might be necessary: gender inequalities in income, opportunities, leisure and other significant outcomes remain and are sometimes sustained by policy, even if there is an observed shift in their character towards support for women's employment (Ferragina and Seeleib-Kaiser 2015).
</p><p>The policy shift has been particularly significant in countries that had previously emphasised more conservative approaches to family policies, such as Germany, Ireland, Japan, and Norway. Hence, it can no longer be assumed that in the majority of rich OECD countries care for young children will be mainly provided through unpaid work within the family. Nevertheless, a certain number of countries still fail to provide adequate childcare arrangements, constituting a barrier for full-time maternal employment. Furthermore, in some countries, such as the United States and the United Kingdom, gender discrimination continues to strongly intersect with class; high childcare costs constitute a disincentive to labour force participation, especially among less educated and unskilled women (Esping-Andersen 2009). This means that higher-class and more educated women tend to have better opportunities than women belonging to a lower social class.
</p><p>Partisanship and women's political agency have been the main drivers for family policy change during the 1980s and 1990s in many countries. For the 2000s, however, the importance of these drivers has significantly declined. As societal preferences have undergone profound changes—to some extent driven by the activities of women's equality movements, as well as by the experience of women's employment—the policy preferences of voters have also changed. Electorates in western democracies increasingly want policies supporting "modern" family lifestyles which depend on women's employment (Ferragina and Seeleib-Kaiser 2015). As political parties react to these changed policy preferences, the traditional differences in family policy positions between political parties decline. The extent to which this translates into support for gender equality, and how such equality might be defined, is as yet not decided. However, societal policy preferences, long believed to be set in stone, are undergoing profound changes; and public opinion increasingly matters for changing policies. The changed policy preferences are also mirrored in new political discourses that prioritise social investment and the preservation of the human capital of women, especially of those who are highly skilled. The expansion of family policies geared to supporting women's employment and investment in children is very likely to continue in western democracies.(Ferragina and Seeleib-Kaiser 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-sp.oxfordjournals.org_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sp.oxfordjournals.org-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While in many parts of the world family policies seek to promote a gender-equal organization of the family life, in others the male-dominated family continues to be the official policy of the authorities, which is also supported by law. For instance, the Civil Code of <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> states at Article 1105: "In relations between husband and wife; the position of the head of the family is the exclusive right of the husband".<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some parts of the world, some governments promote a specific form of family, such as that based on traditional <a href="/wiki/Family_values" title="Family values">family values</a>. The term "family values" is often used in political discourse in some countries, its general meaning being that of traditional or cultural values that pertain to the family's structure, function, roles, beliefs, attitudes, and ideals, usually involving the "traditional family"—a <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle-class</a> family with a breadwinner father and a homemaker mother, raising their biological children. Any deviation from this family model is considered a "nontraditional family".<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> These family ideals are often advanced through policies such as <a href="/wiki/Marriage_promotion" title="Marriage promotion">marriage promotion</a>. Some jurisdictions outlaw practices which they deem as socially or religiously unacceptable, such as <a href="/wiki/Fornication" title="Fornication">fornication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cohabitation" title="Cohabitation">cohabitation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Work-family_balance">Work-family balance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Work-family balance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Work%E2%80%93life_balance" title="Work–life balance">Work–life balance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Work%E2%80%93family_balance_in_the_United_States" title="Work–family balance in the United States">Work–family balance in the United States</a></div>
<p>Work-family balance is a concept involving proper prioritizing between work/career and family life. It includes issues relating to the way how work and families intersect and influence each other. At a political level, it is reflected through policies such <a href="/wiki/Maternity_leave" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternity leave">maternity leave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paternity_leave" class="mw-redirect" title="Paternity leave">paternity leave</a>. Since the 1950s, social scientists as well as feminists have increasingly criticized gendered arrangements of work and care, and the male breadwinner role, and policies are increasingly targeting men as fathers, as a tool of changing gender relations.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protection_of_private_and_family_life">Protection of private and family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Protection of private and family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Article 8 of the <a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a> provides a right to respect for one's "private and family life, his home and his <a href="/wiki/Privacy_of_correspondence" class="mw-redirect" title="Privacy of correspondence">correspondence</a>", subject to certain restrictions that are "in accordance with law" and "necessary in a democratic society".<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="cquote pullquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tbody><tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“
</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;"><b>Article 8 – Right to respect for private and family life</b>
<p>1. Everyone has the right to respect for his private and family life, his home and his correspondence.
</p><p>2. There shall be no interference by a public authority with the exercise of this right except such as is in accordance with the law and is necessary in a democratic society in the interests of national security, public safety or the economic well-being of the country, for the prevention of disorder or crime, for the protection of health or morals, or for the protection of the rights and freedoms of others.
</p>
</td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: &#39;Times New Roman&#39;, Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”
</td></tr>

</tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Certain social scientists have advocated the abolition of the family. An early opponent of the family was Socrates whose position was outlined by Plato in <i>The Republic</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In Book 5 of <i>The Republic</i>, Socrates tells his interlocutors that a just city is one in which citizens have no family ties.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The family being such a deep-rooted and much-venerated institution, few intellectuals have ventured to speak against it. <a href="/wiki/Familialism" title="Familialism">Familialism</a> has been atypically defined as a “social structure where … a family's values are held in higher esteem than the values of the individual members of the family.”<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/In-group_favoritism" title="In-group favoritism">Favoritism</a> granted to relatives regardless of <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/merit" class="extiw" title="wikt:merit">merit</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a>.
</p><p>The Russian-American <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualist</a> philosopher, novelist and playwright <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a> compared partiality towards <a href="/wiki/Consanguinity" title="Consanguinity">consanguinity</a> with <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, as a small-scale manifestation of the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-rand_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rand-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> “The worship of the family is merely racism, like a crudely primitive first installment on the worship of the tribe. It places the <a href="/wiki/Accident_of_birth" title="Accident of birth">accident of birth</a> above a man's values and duty to the tribe above a man's right to his own life.”<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, she spoke in favor of <a href="/wiki/Childfree" class="mw-redirect" title="Childfree">childfree</a> lifestyle, while following it herself.<sup id="cite_ref-rand_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rand-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The British <a href="/wiki/Social_criticism" title="Social criticism">social critic</a>, poet, mountaineer and occultist <a href="/wiki/Aleister_Crowley" title="Aleister Crowley">Aleister Crowley</a> censured the institution of family in his works: “Horrid word, family!  Its very <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> accuses it of servility and stagnation. / Latin, <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/famulus" class="extiw" title="wikt:famulus">famulus</a></i>, a servant; Oscan, <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Faamat" class="extiw" title="wikt:Faamat">Faamat</a></i>, he dwells. … [T]hink what horrid images it evokes from the mind.  Not only <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a>; wherever the family has been strong, it has always been an engine of tyranny.  Weak members or weak neighbours: it is the mob spirit crushing <a href="/wiki/Holy_Guardian_Angel" title="Holy Guardian Angel">genius</a>, or overwhelming opposition by brute arithmetic. … In every <a href="/wiki/Magick_(Thelema)" title="Magick (Thelema)">Magical</a>, or similar system, it is invariably the first condition which the Aspirant must fulfill: he must once and for all and for ever put his family outside his magical circle.”<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_family_and_social_justice">The family and social justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: The family and social justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>One of the controversies regarding the family is the application of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a> to the private sphere of family relations, in particular with regard to the <a href="/wiki/Rights_of_women" class="mw-redirect" title="Rights of women">rights of women</a> and <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_rights" title="Children&#39;s rights">children</a>. Throughout much of the history, most philosophers who advocated for social justice focused on the public political arena, not on the family structures; with the family often being seen as a separate entity which needed to be protected from outside state intrusion. One notable exception was <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, who, in his work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Subjection_of_Women" title="The Subjection of Women">The Subjection of Women</a></i>, advocated for greater rights for women within marriage and family.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Second wave feminists argued that <a href="/wiki/The_personal_is_political" title="The personal is political">the personal is political</a>, stating that there are strong connections between personal experiences and the larger social and political structures. In the context of the feminist movement of the 1960s and 1970s, this was a challenge to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> and <a href="/wiki/Family_values" title="Family values">family values</a>, as they were understood then.<sup id="cite_ref-Harutyunyan2009p50_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harutyunyan2009p50-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Feminists focused on <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, arguing that the reluctance—in law or in practice—of the state to intervene and offer protection to women who have been abused within the family, is in violation of women's <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, and is the result of an ideology which places family relations outside the conceptual framework of human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_trends_in_family_composition">Global trends in family composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Family&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Global trends in family composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2015, Nicholas Eberstadt, political economist at the <a href="/wiki/American_Enterprise_Institute" title="American Enterprise Institute">American Enterprise Institute</a> in Washington, described a "global flight from family" in an opinion piece in the <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_Eberstadt_2015_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_Eberstadt_2015-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Statistics from an infographic by Olivier Ballou showed that,<sup id="cite_ref-AEI_2015_Eberstadt_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AEI_2015_Eberstadt-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In 2013, just over 40% of US babies were born outside marriage. The Census bureau estimated that 27% of all children lived in a fatherless home. Europe has seen a surge in child-free adults. One in five 40-something women are childless in Sweden and in Switzerland, in Italy one in four, in Berlin one in three. So-called traditional societies are seeing the same trend. About one-sixth of Japanese women in their forties have never married and about 30% of all woman that age are childless.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.aei.org/multimedia/global-flight-family">Infographic Olivier Ballou (AEI)</a></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>However, Swedish statisticians reported in 2013 that, in contrast to many countries, since the 2000s, fewer children have experienced their parents' separation, childlessness had decreased in Sweden and marriages had increased. It had also become more common for couples to have a third child suggesting that the nuclear family was no longer in decline in Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-unece_2013_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unece_2013-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>10</span></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We have seen that people can refer to their relatives as 'the family.' 'All the family turned up for the funeral... But of course, my brother didn't bring his family along— they're much too young.' Here the reference is to the offspring (as distinct from 'all' the family). The neighbors were very good, too. 'The Jones came, and their two children. It was nice, the whole family turning up like that.' Here the usage is more restricted than 'relatives' or 'his relatives,' but includes just both parents and offspring. 'Of course, the children will be leaving home soon. It's always sad to see the family break up like that.' Here the reference is not only to parents and children but to their co-residence, that is, to the household.<i>The Family and Industrial Society</i>, 1983, George Allen Unwin, London, p. 30</span>

<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By replacement in the definition of the notion of "generation" by <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>". Since identical twins are not separated by meiosis, there are no "generations" between them, hence <i>n</i>=0 and <i>r</i>=1. See <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.genetic-genealogy.co.uk/Toc115570135.html">genetic-genealogy.co.uk</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although married women in France obtained the right to work without their husbands' permission in 1965,<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304092212/http://www.tandf.co.uk/journals/explore/cmcf-vsi-women-in-france.pdf">"Archived copy"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2016-04-03</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.tandf.co.uk%2Fjournals%2Fexplore%2Fcmcf-vsi-women-in-france.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFamily" class="Z3988"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: archived copy as title (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_archived_copy_as_title" title="Category:CS1 maint: archived copy as title">link</a>)</span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/> and the paternal authority of a man over his family was ended in 1970 (before that parental responsibilities belonged solely to the father who made all legal decisions concerning the children), it was only in 1985 that a legal reform abolished the stipulation that the husband had the sole power to administer the children's property. <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://ceflonline.net/wp-content/uploads/France-Parental-Responsibilities.pdf">[2]</a></span>
